<p><b>Environmentalism</b> or <b>environmental rights</b> is a broad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_life" title="Philosophy of life">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Social_movement" title="Social movement">social movement</a> regarding concerns for <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">environmental protection</a> and improvement of the health of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>, particularly as the measure for this health seeks to incorporate the impact of changes to the environment on humans, animals, plants and non-living matter.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Circular_definition" title="Circular definition"><span title="A term's definition must use terms other than that being defined.">circular definition</span></a></i>]</sup> While environmentalism focuses more on the environmental and nature-related aspects of <a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">green ideology and politics</a>, ecologism combines the ideology of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_ecology_(theory)" title="Social ecology (theory)">social ecology</a> and environmentalism. <i>Ecologism</i> is more commonly used in continental European languages, while <i>environmentalism</i> is more commonly used in English but the words have slightly different connotations.
</p><p>Environmentalism advocates the preservation, restoration and improvement of the natural environment and critical <a href="/wiki/Earth_system_science" title="Earth system science">earth system</a> elements or processes such as the <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, and may be referred to as a movement to control <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> or protect plant and animal diversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> For this reason, concepts such as a <a href="/wiki/Land_ethic" title="Land ethic">land ethic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_ethics" title="Environmental ethics">environmental ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Biophilia_hypothesis" title="Biophilia hypothesis">biophilia hypothesis</a> figure predominantly.
</p><p>At its crux, environmentalism is an attempt to balance relations between humans and the various natural systems on which they depend in such a way that all the components are accorded a proper degree of <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>. The exact measures and outcomes of this balance is controversial and there are many different ways for environmental concerns to be expressed in practice. Environmentalism and environmental concerns are often represented by the colour <a href="/wiki/Green#In_culture" title="Green">green</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Light,_Dark_and_Bright_Green_Environmentalism_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Light,_Dark_and_Bright_Green_Environmentalism-2">[2]</a></sup> but this association has been appropriated by the marketing industries for the tactic known as <a href="/wiki/Greenwashing" title="Greenwashing">greenwashing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Environmentalism is opposed by <a href="/wiki/Anti-environmentalism" title="Anti-environmentalism">anti-environmentalism</a>, which says that the Earth is less fragile than some environmentalists maintain, and portrays environmentalism as overreacting to the human contribution to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> or opposing human advancement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rowell_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-rowell-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Environmentalism</i> denotes a <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">social movement</a> that seeks to influence the political process by lobbying, activism, and education in order to protect natural resources and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>.
</p><p>An <i><a href="/wiki/Environmentalist" title="Environmentalist">environmentalist</a></i> is a person who may speak out about our natural environment and the sustainable management of its resources through changes in public policy or individual behaviour. This may include supporting practices such as informed consumption, conservation initiatives, investment in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_resources" title="Renewable resources">renewable resources</a>, improved efficiencies in the materials economy, transitioning to new accounting paradigms such as <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">ecological economics</a>, renewing and revitalizing our connections with non-human life or even opting to have one less child to reduce consumption and pressure on resources.
</p><p>In various ways (for example, grassroots activism and protests), environmentalists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_organization" title="Environmental organization">environmental organisations</a> seek to give the natural world a stronger voice in human affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general terms, environmentalists advocate the <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable</a> management of resources, and the protection (and restoration, when necessary) of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural environment</a> through changes in public policy and individual behaviour. In its recognition of humanity as a participant in ecosystems, the movement is centered around <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">Conservation movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_history_of_environmentalism" title="Timeline of history of environmentalism">Timeline of history of environmentalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mahaveer_swami.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="285" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Mahaveer_swami.jpg/170px-Mahaveer_swami.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b1/Mahaveer_swami.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mahaveer_swami.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lord <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a>, the last <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jain</a> Tirthankar, is also considered to be a great environmentalist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A concern for environmental protection has recurred in diverse forms, in different parts of the world, throughout history.
The earliest ideas of environmental protectionism can be found in <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, a religion from ancient India revived by <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a> in the 6th century BC. Jainism offers a view that is in many ways compatible with core values associated with environmental activism, such as the protection of life by <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">nonviolence</a>, which could form a strong ecological ethos for global protection of the environment. Mahavira's teachings on the symbiosis between all living beings—as well as the five elements of earth, water, air, fire, and space—are core to environmental thought today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Middle East, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> in the 630s AD commanded his army to "Bring no harm to the trees, nor burn them with fire," and to "Slay not any of the enemy's flock, save for your food."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuhur_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zuhur-9">[9]</a></sup> Various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_medicine" title="Islamic medicine">Arabic medical</a> treatises during the 9th to 13th centuries dealt with environmentalism and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a>, including the issue of pollution. The authors of such treatises included <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qusta_ibn_Luqa" title="Qusta ibn Luqa">Qusta ibn Luqa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Al-Razi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Al-Jazzar" title="Ibn Al-Jazzar">Ibn Al-Jazzar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Tamimi" title="Al-Tamimi">al-Tamimi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Masihi" title="Al-Masihi">al-Masihi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Ridwan" title="Ali ibn Ridwan">Ali ibn Ridwan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Jumay" title="Ibn Jumay">Ibn Jumay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Israeli_ben_Solomon" title="Isaac Israeli ben Solomon">Isaac Israeli ben Solomon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd_al-Latif_al-Baghdadi_(medieval_writer)" title="Abd al-Latif al-Baghdadi (medieval writer)">Abd-el-latif</a>, Ibn al-Quff, and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nafis" title="Ibn al-Nafis">Ibn al-Nafis</a>. Their works covered a number of subjects related to pollution, such as air pollution, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">soil contamination</a>, and the mishandling of <a href="/wiki/Municipal_solid_waste" title="Municipal solid waste">municipal solid waste</a>. They also included <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_assessment" title="Environmental impact assessment">asessments</a> of certain localities' environmental impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Europe, King <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a> banned the burning and sale of "<a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">sea-coal</a>" in 1272 by proclamation in London, after its smoke had become a prevalent annoyance throughout the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pea-souper_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pea-souper-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deadly_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deadly-12">[12]</a></sup> This fuel, common in London due to the local scarcity of wood, was given this early name because it could be found washed up on some shores, from where it was carted away on a wheelbarrow.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_environmental_legislation">Early environmental legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early environmental legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="653" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg/220px-StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg/330px-StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg/440px-StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StRolloxChemical_1831.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Levels of air pollution rose during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, sparking the first modern environmental laws to be passed in the mid-19th century.</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>
At the advent of steam and electricity the muse of history holds her nose and shuts her eyes (<a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> 1918).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The origins of the environmental movement lay in the response to increasing levels of <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a> <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. The emergence of great factories and the concomitant immense growth in <a href="/wiki/History_of_coal_mining" title="History of coal mining">coal consumption</a> gave rise to an unprecedented level of <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> in industrial centers; after 1900 the large volume of industrial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> discharges added to the growing load of untreated human waste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donora_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donora-14">[14]</a></sup> The first large-scale, modern environmental laws came in the form of Britain's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_Acts" title="Alkali Acts">Alkali Acts</a>, passed in 1863, to regulate the deleterious air pollution (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaseous" title="Gaseous">gaseous</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>) given off by the <a href="/wiki/Leblanc_process" title="Leblanc process">Leblanc process</a>, used to produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_ash" title="Soda ash">soda ash</a>. An Alkali inspector and four sub-inspectors were appointed to curb this pollution. The inspectorate's responsibilities were gradually expanded, culminating in the Alkali Order 1958 which placed all major heavy industries that emitted <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a>, grit, dust and fumes under supervision.
</p><p>In industrial cities, local experts and reformers, especially after 1890, took the lead in identifying <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a> and pollution, and initiating grass-roots movements to demand and achieve reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Typically the highest priority went to water and air pollution. The <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_UK" title="Environmental Protection UK">Coal Smoke Abatement Society</a> was formed in 1898 making it one of the oldest environmental NGOs. It was founded by artist Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Blake_Richmond" title="William Blake Richmond">William Blake Richmond</a>, frustrated with the pall cast by coal smoke. Although there were earlier pieces of legislation, the <a href="/wiki/Public_Health_Act_1875" title="Public Health Act 1875">Public Health Act 1875</a> required all furnaces and fireplaces to consume their own smoke. It also provided for sanctions against factories that emitted large amounts of black smoke. This law's provisions were extended in 1926 with the Smoke Abatement Act to include other emissions, such as soot, ash, and gritty particles, and to empower local authorities to impose their own regulations.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Revolution_of_1936" title="Spanish Revolution of 1936">Spanish Revolution</a>, anarchist-controlled territories undertook several environmental reforms, which were possibly the largest in the world at the time. <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Gu%C3%A9rin" title="Daniel Guérin">Daniel Guerin</a> notes that <a href="/wiki/List_of_anarchist_communities" title="List of anarchist communities">anarchist territories</a> would diversify crops, extend <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>, initiate <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a>, start tree nurseries and help to establish <a href="/wiki/Naturist_resort" title="Naturist resort">naturist communities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Once there was a link discovered between air pollution and tuberculosis, the <a href="/wiki/Confederaci%C3%B3n_Nacional_del_Trabajo" title="Confederación Nacional del Trabajo">CNT</a> shut down several metal factories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was only under the impetus of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Smog" title="Great Smog">Great Smog</a> of 1952 in London, which almost brought the city to a standstill and may have caused upward of 6,000 deaths, that the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_1956" title="Clean Air Act 1956">Clean Air Act 1956</a> was passed and airborne pollution in the city was first tackled. Financial incentives were offered to householders to replace open coal fires with alternatives (such as installing gas fires) or those who preferred, to burn coke instead (a byproduct of town gas production) which produces minimal smoke. 'Smoke control areas' were introduced in some towns and cities where only smokeless fuels could be burnt and power stations were relocated away from cities. The act formed an important impetus to modern environmentalism and caused a rethinking of the dangers of environmental degradation to people's quality of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The late 19th century also saw the passage of the first wildlife conservation laws.
The zoologist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Newton" title="Alfred Newton">Alfred Newton</a> published a series of investigations into the <i>Desirability of establishing a 'Close-time' for the preservation of indigenous animals</i> between 1872 and 1903. His advocacy for legislation to protect animals from hunting during the mating season led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a> and influenced the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Birds_Preservation_Act_1869" title="Sea Birds Preservation Act 1869">Sea Birds Preservation Act</a> in 1869 as the first nature protection law in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_environmental_movements">First environmental movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First environmental movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early interest in the environment was a feature of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantic_movement" title="Romantic movement">Romantic movement</a> in the early 19th century. One of the earliest modern pronouncements on thinking about human industrial advancement and its influence on the environment was written by Japanese geographer, educator, philosopher and author <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsunesaburo_Makiguchi" title="Tsunesaburo Makiguchi">Tsunesaburo Makiguchi</a> in his 1903 publication <i>Jinsei Chirigaku</i> (<i>A Geography of Human Life</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odata1994_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Odata1994-21">[21]</a></sup> In Britain the poet <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a> travelled extensively in the <a href="/wiki/Lake_District" title="Lake District">Lake District</a> and wrote that it is a "sort of national property in which every man has a right and interest who has an eye to perceive and a heart to enjoy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="437" data-file-width="370" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg/170px-John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg/255px-John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg/340px-John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Ruskin_-_Portrait_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_17774.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>, an influential thinker who articulated the Romantic ideal of environmental protection and conservation</div></div></div>
<p>Systematic efforts on behalf of the environment only began in the late 19th century; it grew out of the amenity movement in Britain in the 1870s, which was a reaction to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">industrialisation</a>, the growth of cities, and worsening air and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>. Starting with the formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commons_Preservation_Society" title="Commons Preservation Society">Commons Preservation Society</a> in 1865, the movement championed rural preservation against the encroachments of industrialisation. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Hunter_(National_Trust)" title="Robert Hunter (National Trust)">Robert Hunter</a>, solicitor for the society, worked with <a href="/wiki/Hardwicke_Rawnsley" title="Hardwicke Rawnsley">Hardwicke Rawnsley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Octavia_Hill" title="Octavia Hill">Octavia Hill</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a> to lead a successful campaign to prevent the construction of railways to carry slate from the quarries, which would have ruined the unspoiled valleys of <a href="/wiki/Newlands_Valley" title="Newlands Valley">Newlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ennerdale_Water" title="Ennerdale Water">Ennerdale</a>. This success led to the formation of the Lake District Defence Society (later to become The Friends of the Lake District).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vc_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-vc-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a> wrote about ecology in <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">agricultural science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>, criminology, urban planning, geography, geology and biology. He observed in Swiss and Siberian glaciers that they had been slowly melting since the dawn of the industrial revolution, possibly making him one of the first predictors for climate change. He also observed the damage done from deforestation and hunting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Kropotkin's writings would become influential in the 1970s and became a major inspiration for the <a href="/wiki/Intentional_community" title="Intentional community">intentional community</a> movement as well as his ideas becoming the basis for the theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_ecology_(theory)" title="Social ecology (theory)">social ecology</a>.
</p><p>In 1893 Hill, Hunter and Rawnsley agreed to set up a national body to coordinate environmental conservation efforts across the country; the "National Trust for Places of Historic Interest or Natural Beauty" was formally inaugurated in 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-t94_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-t94-25">[25]</a></sup> The organisation obtained secure footing through the 1907 National Trust Bill, which gave the trust the status of a statutory corporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and the bill was passed in August 1907.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-act_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-act-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>An early "Back-to-Nature" movement, which anticipated the romantic ideal of modern environmentalism, was advocated by intellectuals such as <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Carpenter" title="Edward Carpenter">Edward Carpenter</a>, who were all against <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> and other activities that were harmful to the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The movement was a reaction to the urban conditions of the industrial towns, where sanitation was awful, pollution levels intolerable and housing terribly cramped. Idealists championed the rural life as a mythical <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopia</a> and advocated a return to it. John Ruskin argued that people should return to a "small piece of English ground, beautiful, peaceful, and fruitful. We will have no steam engines upon it ... we will have plenty of flowers and vegetables ... we will have some music and poetry; the children will learn to dance to it and sing it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Practical ventures in the establishment of small cooperative farms were even attempted and old rural traditions, without the "taint of manufacture or the canker of artificiality", were enthusiastically revived, including the <a href="/wiki/Morris_dance" title="Morris dance">Morris dance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maypole" title="Maypole">maypole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>These ideas also inspired various environmental groups in the UK, such as the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a>, established in 1889 by <a href="/wiki/Emily_Williamson" title="Emily Williamson">Emily Williamson</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Advocacy_group" title="Advocacy group">protest group</a> to campaign for greater protection for the indigenous birds of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">island</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The Society attracted growing support from the suburban middle-classes as well as support from many other influential figures, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ornithologist" title="Ornithologist">ornithologist</a> Professor <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Newton" title="Alfred Newton">Alfred Newton</a>. By 1900, public support for the organisation had grown, and it had over 25,000 members. The <a href="/wiki/Garden_city_movement" title="Garden city movement">garden city movement</a> incorporated many environmental concerns into its <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> manifesto; the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_League_(UK,_1885)" title="Socialist League (UK, 1885)">Socialist League</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Clarion" title="The Clarion">The Clarion</a> movement also began to advocate measures of <a href="/wiki/Nature_conservation" title="Nature conservation">nature conservation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walden_Thoreau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2292" data-file-width="1360" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Walden_Thoreau.jpg/170px-Walden_Thoreau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Walden_Thoreau.jpg/255px-Walden_Thoreau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Walden_Thoreau.jpg/340px-Walden_Thoreau.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walden_Thoreau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original title page of <i><a href="/wiki/Walden" title="Walden">Walden</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement_in_the_United_States" title="Environmental movement in the United States">The movement in the United States</a> began in the late 19th century, out of concerns for protecting the natural resources of the West, with individuals such as <a href="/wiki/John_Muir" title="John Muir">John Muir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a> making key philosophical contributions. Thoreau was interested in peoples' relationship with nature and studied this by living close to nature in a simple life. He published his experiences in the book <i><a href="/wiki/Walden" title="Walden">Walden</a></i>, which argues that people should become intimately close with nature. Muir came to believe in nature's inherent right, especially after spending time hiking in <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_Valley" title="Yosemite Valley">Yosemite Valley</a> and studying both the ecology and geology. He successfully lobbied congress to form <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_National_Park" title="Yosemite National Park">Yosemite National Park</a> and went on to set up the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Club" title="Sierra Club">Sierra Club</a> in 1892. The conservationist principles as well as the belief in an inherent right of nature were to become the bedrock of modern environmentalism.
</p><p>In the 20th century, environmental ideas continued to grow in popularity and recognition. Efforts were starting to be made to save some wildlife, particularly the <a href="/wiki/American_bison" title="American bison">American bison</a>. The death of the last <a href="/wiki/Passenger_pigeon" title="Passenger pigeon">passenger pigeon</a> as well as the endangerment of the American bison helped to focus the minds of conservationists and to popularise their concerns. In 1916, the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> was founded by US President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Forestry_Commission" title="Forestry Commission">Forestry Commission</a> was set up in 1919 in Britain to increase the amount of woodland in Britain by buying land for <a href="/wiki/Afforestation" title="Afforestation">afforestation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a>. The commission was also tasked with promoting forestry and the production of timber for trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woodland_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-woodland-33">[33]</a></sup> During the 1920s the Commission focused on acquiring land to begin planting out new forests; much of the land was previously used for agricultural purposes. By 1939 the Forestry Commission was the largest landowner in Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ForestPoliciesBook_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ForestPoliciesBook-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Nazi_Germany"></span>
During the 1930s the Nazis had elements that were supportive of animal rights, zoos and wildlife,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BHTFSN153_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-BHTFSN153-35">[35]</a></sup> and took several measures to ensure their protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMG278_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-HMG278-36">[36]</a></sup> In 1933 the government created a stringent animal-protection law and in 1934, <i>Das Reichsjagdgesetz</i> (The Reich Hunting Law) was enacted which limited hunting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www_kaltio_fi5_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-www_kaltio_fi5-38">[38]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmentalism_in_Nazi_Germany" title="Environmentalism in Nazi Germany">Several Nazis were environmentalists</a> (notably <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Hess" title="Rudolf Hess">Rudolf Hess</a>), and species protection and <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> were significant issues in the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMG278_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-HMG278-36">[36]</a></sup> In 1935, the regime enacted the "Reich Nature Protection Act" (<i>Reichsnaturschutzgesetz</i>). The concept of the <i>Dauerwald</i> (best translated as the "perpetual forest") which included concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Forest_management" title="Forest management">forest management</a> and protection was promoted and efforts were also made to curb <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1949, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Sand_County_Almanac" title="A Sand County Almanac">A Sand County Almanac</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Aldo_Leopold" title="Aldo Leopold">Aldo Leopold</a> was published. It explained Leopold's belief that humankind should have moral respect for the environment and that it is unethical to harm it. The book is sometimes called the most influential book on conservation.
</p><p>Throughout the 1950s, 1960s, 1970s and beyond, photography was used to enhance public awareness of the need for protecting land and recruiting members to environmental organisations. <a href="/wiki/David_Brower" title="David Brower">David Brower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ansel_Adams" title="Ansel Adams">Ansel Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Newhall" title="Nancy Newhall">Nancy Newhall</a> created the Sierra Club Exhibit Format Series, which helped raise public environmental awareness and brought a rapidly increasing flood of new members to the Sierra Club and to the environmental movement in general. <i>This Is Dinosaur</i>, edited by <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Stegner" title="Wallace Stegner">Wallace Stegner</a> with photographs by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Litton_(environmentalist)" title="Martin Litton (environmentalist)">Martin Litton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hyde_(photographer)" title="Philip Hyde (photographer)">Philip Hyde</a>, prevented the building of dams within <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_National_Monument" title="Dinosaur National Monument">Dinosaur National Monument</a> by becoming part of a new kind of activism called environmentalism that combined the conservationist ideals of Thoreau, Leopold and Muir with hard-hitting advertising, lobbying, book distribution, letter writing campaigns, and more. The powerful use of photography in addition to the written word for conservation dated back to the creation of <a href="/wiki/Yosemite_National_Park" title="Yosemite National Park">Yosemite National Park</a>, when photographs persuaded Abraham Lincoln to preserve the beautiful glacier carved landscape for all time. The Sierra Club Exhibit Format Series galvanised public opposition to building dams in the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a> and protected many other national treasures. The Sierra Club often led a coalition of many environmental groups including the <a href="/wiki/The_Wilderness_Society_(United_States)" title="The Wilderness Society (United States)">Wilderness Society</a> and many others.
</p><p>After a focus on preserving wilderness in the 1950s and 1960s, the Sierra Club and other groups broadened their focus to include such issues as air and water pollution, population concern, and curbing the <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources" title="Exploitation of natural resources">exploitation of natural resources</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_expansion">Post-war expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Post-war expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Steady-state_economy#Post-war_economic_expansion_and_emerging_ecological_concerns" title="Steady-state economy">Steady-state economy  § Post-war economic expansion and emerging ecological concerns</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/220px-Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/330px-Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/440px-Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suburbia_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the United States and several other countries, the boom was manifested in suburban development and <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a>, aided by automobile ownership.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1962, <i><a href="/wiki/Silent_Spring" title="Silent Spring">Silent Spring</a></i> by American biologist <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson" title="Rachel Carson">Rachel Carson</a> was published. The book cataloged the environmental impacts of the indiscriminate spraying of <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> in the US and questioned the logic of releasing large amounts of chemicals into the environment without fully understanding their effects on human health and ecology. The book suggested that DDT and other pesticides may cause <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> and that their agricultural use was a threat to wildlife, particularly birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silent_Spring_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silent_Spring-40">[40]</a></sup> The resulting public concern led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> in 1970 which subsequently banned the agricultural use of DDT in the US in 1972. The limited use of DDT in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">disease vector</a> control continues to this day in certain parts of the world and remains controversial. The book's legacy was to produce a far greater awareness of environmental issues and interest into how people affect the environment. With this new interest in environment came interest in problems such as air pollution and petroleum spills, and environmental interest grew. New pressure groups formed, notably <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friends_of_the_Earth_(US)" title="Friends of the Earth (US)">Friends of the Earth (US)</a>, as well as notable local organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/Wyoming_Outdoor_Council" title="Wyoming Outdoor Council">Wyoming Outdoor Council</a>, which was founded in 1967.
</p><p>In the 1970s, the environmental movement gained rapid speed around the world as a productive outgrowth of the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">counterculture</a> movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world's first political parties to campaign on a predominantly environmental platform were the <a href="/wiki/United_Tasmania_Group" title="United Tasmania Group">United Tasmania Group</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania, Australia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Values_Party" title="Values Party">Values Party</a> of New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dann,_Christine_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dann,_Christine-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bevan,_RA_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bevan,_RA-43">[43]</a></sup> The first green party in Europe was the Popular Movement for the Environment, founded in 1972 in the Swiss canton of <a href="/wiki/Neuch%C3%A2tel" title="Neuchâtel">Neuchâtel</a>. The first national green party in Europe was PEOPLE, founded in Britain in February 1973, which eventually turned into the <a href="/wiki/Ecology_Party" title="Ecology Party">Ecology Party</a>, and then the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_(UK)" title="Green Party (UK)">Green Party</a>.
</p><p>Protection of the environment also became important in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Chipko_movement" title="Chipko movement">Chipko movement</a> was formed in India under the influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi" title="Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi">Mhatmas Gandhi</a> and they set up peaceful resistance to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> by literally hugging trees (leading to the term "tree huggers"). Their peaceful methods of protest and slogan "ecology is permanent economy" were very influential.
</p><p>Another milestone in the movement was the creation of <a href="/wiki/Earth_Day" title="Earth Day">Earth Day</a>. Earth Day was first observed in San Francisco and other cities on 21 March 1970, the first day of spring. It was created to give awareness to environmental issues. On 21 March 1971, United Nations Secretary-General U Thant spoke of a <a href="/wiki/Spaceship_Earth" title="Spaceship Earth">spaceship Earth</a> on Earth Day, hereby referring to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> the earth supplies to us, and hence our obligation to protect it (and with it, ourselves). Earth Day is now coordinated globally by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_Day_Network" title="Earth Day Network">Earth Day Network</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> and is celebrated in more than 192 countries every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The UN's first major conference on international environmental issues, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_the_Human_Environment" title="United Nations Conference on the Human Environment">United Nations Conference on the Human Environment</a> (also known as the Stockholm Conference), was held on 5–16 June 1972. It marked a turning point in the development of international environmental politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baylis3_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baylis3-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-1970s, many felt that people were on the edge of environmental catastrophe. The <a href="/wiki/Back-to-the-land_movement" title="Back-to-the-land movement">back-to-the-land movement</a> started to form and ideas of environmental ethics joined with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Vietnam_War" title="Anti-Vietnam War">anti-Vietnam War</a> sentiments and other political issues. These individuals lived outside normal society and started to take on some of the more radical environmental theories such as <a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">deep ecology</a>. Around this time more mainstream environmentalism was starting to show force with the signing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act" title="Endangered Species Act">Endangered Species Act</a> in 1973 and the formation of <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a> in 1975. Significant amendments were also enacted to the United States <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1979, <a href="/wiki/James_Lovelock" title="James Lovelock">James Lovelock</a>, a British scientist, published <i>Gaia: A new look at life on Earth</i>, which put forth the <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis">Gaia hypothesis</a>; it proposes that life on earth can be understood as a single organism. This became an important part of the <a href="/wiki/Deep_Green_Resistance" title="Deep Green Resistance">Deep Green</a> ideology. Throughout the rest of the history of environmentalism there has been debate and argument between more radical followers of this Deep Green ideology and more mainstream environmentalists.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century_and_beyond">21st century and beyond</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 21st century and beyond">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Environmentalism continues to evolve to face up to new issues such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic pollution</a>.
</p><p>Research demonstrates a precipitous decline in the US public's interest in 19 different areas of environmental concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Americans are less likely to be actively participating in an environmental movement or organisation and more likely to identify as "unsympathetic" to an environmental movement than in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gallup.com_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-gallup.com-50">[50]</a></sup> This is likely a lingering factor of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> in 2008. Since 2005, the percentage of Americans agreeing that the environment should be given priority over economic growth has dropped 10 points; in contrast, those feeling that growth should be given priority "even if the environment suffers to some extent" has risen 12 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gallup.com_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-gallup.com-50">[50]</a></sup> Nevertheless, a recent <i>National Geographic</i> survey indicated strong desire for commitment across a dozen countries, indicating a majority were in favour of more than half of the Earth's land surface being protected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_forms_of_ecoactivism">New forms of ecoactivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: New forms of ecoactivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="1463" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg/170px-Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg/255px-Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg/340px-Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Berkeley_Tree_Sitters_-_UC_Berkeley_Memorial_Oak_Grove.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Demonstrators in a tree at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley_oak_grove_controversy" title="University of California, Berkeley oak grove controversy">Berkeley oak grove protest</a> in 2008</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tree_sitting" title="Tree sitting">Tree sitting</a> is a form of activism in which the protester sits in a tree in an attempt to stop the removal of a tree or to impede the demolition of an area with the longest and most famous tree-sitter being <a href="/wiki/Julia_Butterfly_Hill" title="Julia Butterfly Hill">Julia Butterfly Hill</a>, who spent 738 days in a California Redwood, saving a three-acre tract of forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Also notable is the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Finch_tree_sit" title="Yellow Finch tree sit">Yellow Finch tree sit</a>, which was a 932-day blockade of the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Valley_Pipeline" title="Mountain Valley Pipeline">Mountain Valley Pipeline</a> from 2018-2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sit-in" title="Sit-in">Sit-ins</a> can be used to encourage social change, such as the Greensboro sit-ins, a series of protests in 1960 to stop racial segregation, but can also be used in ecoactivism, as in the <a href="/wiki/Dakota_Access_Pipeline" title="Dakota Access Pipeline">Dakota Access Pipeline</a> Protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rojava" title="Rojava">Rojava</a> had been ecologically damaged by <a href="/wiki/Monoculture" title="Monoculture">monoculture</a>, oil extraction, damming of rivers, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">topsoil loss</a> and general pollution. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Democratic_Federation_of_Northern_Syria" title="Democratic Federation of Northern Syria">DFNS</a> launched a campaign titled 'Make Rojava Green Again' (a parody of <a href="/wiki/Make_America_Great_Again" title="Make America Great Again">Make America Great Again</a>) which is attempting to provide <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> to communities (especially <a href="/wiki/Solar_Energy_Generating_Systems" title="Solar Energy Generating Systems">solar energy</a>), <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a>, protecting water sources, planting gardens, promoting <a href="/wiki/Urban_agriculture" title="Urban agriculture">urban agriculture</a>, creating <a href="/wiki/Nature_reserve" title="Nature reserve">wildlife reserves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reclaimed_water" title="Reclaimed water">water recycling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beekeeping" title="Beekeeping">beekeeping</a>, expanding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_Transportation" title="Public Transportation">public transportation</a> and promoting environmental awareness within their communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rebel_Zapatista_Autonomous_Municipalities" title="Rebel Zapatista Autonomous Municipalities">Rebel Zapatista Autonomous Municipalities</a> are firmly environmentalist and have stopped the extraction of oil, uranium, timber and metal from the <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_Jungle" title="Lacandon Jungle">Lacandon Jungle</a> and stopped the use of <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">chemical fertilisers</a> in farming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIPO-RFM" title="CIPO-RFM">CIPO-RFM</a> has engaged in <a href="/wiki/Sabotage" title="Sabotage">sabotage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Direct_action" title="Direct action">direct action</a> against <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shrimp_farms" title="Shrimp farms">shrimp farms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eucalyptus" title="Eucalyptus">eucalyptus plantations</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timber_industry" title="Timber industry">timber industry</a>. They have also set up corn and coffee <a href="/wiki/Worker_cooperative" title="Worker cooperative">worker cooperatives</a> and built schools and hospitals to help the local populations. They have also created a network of autonomous community radio stations to educate people about dangers to the environment and inform the surrounding communities about new industrial projects that would destroy more land. In 2001, the CIPO-RFM defeated the construction of a highway that was part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plan_Puebla_Panama" title="Plan Puebla Panama">Plan Puebla Panama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_movement">Environmental movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Environmental movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">Environmental movement</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg/220px-Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg/330px-Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg/440px-Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Before <a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_desulfurization" title="Flue-gas desulfurization">flue-gas desulfurization</a> was installed, the <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air-polluting</a> emissions from this power plant in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> contained excessive amounts of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">environmental movement</a></i> (a term that sometimes includes the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">green</a> movements) is a diverse scientific, <a href="/wiki/Social_movement" title="Social movement">social</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Political_movement" title="Political movement">political movement</a>. Though the movement is represented by a range of organisations, because of the inclusion of environmentalism in the classroom curriculum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craig_Kridel_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craig_Kridel-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LessonPlanet-Sinsel_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-LessonPlanet-Sinsel-60">[60]</a></sup> the environmental movement has a younger demographic than is common in other social movements (see <a href="/wiki/Green_seniors" title="Green seniors">green seniors</a>).
</p><p>Environmentalism as a movement covers broad areas of institutional oppression, including for example: consumption of ecosystems and natural resources into waste, dumping waste into disadvantaged communities, air pollution, water pollution, weak infrastructure, exposure of organic life to toxins, mono-culture, anti-polythene drive (jhola movement) and various other focuses. Because of these divisions, the environmental movement can be categorized into these primary focuses: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a>, environmental activism, environmental advocacy, and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_justice" title="Environmental justice">environmental justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_market_environmentalism">Free market environmentalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Free market environmentalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Free-market_environmentalism" title="Free-market environmentalism">Free-market environmentalism</a></div>
<p>Free market environmentalism is a theory that argues that the <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Property_rights" title="Property rights">property rights</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tort" title="Tort">tort</a> law provide the best tools to preserve the health and <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> of the environment. It considers environmental stewardship to be natural, as well as the expulsion of polluters and other aggressors through individual and <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evangelical_environmentalism">Evangelical environmentalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Evangelical environmentalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_environmentalism" title="Evangelical environmentalism">Evangelical environmentalism</a></div>
<p>Evangelical environmentalism is an environmental movement in the United States in which some <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelicals</a> have emphasized <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">biblical</a> mandates concerning humanity's role as steward and subsequent responsibility for the care taking of Creation. While the movement has focused on different environmental issues, it is best known for its focus of addressing climate action from a biblically grounded <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theological</a> perspective. This movement is controversial among some non-Christian environmentalists due to its rooting in a specific religion.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preservation_and_conservation">Preservation and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Preservation and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations,_1987.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5461" data-file-width="7622" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations%2C_1987.jpg/220px-An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations%2C_1987.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations%2C_1987.jpg/330px-An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations%2C_1987.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations%2C_1987.jpg/440px-An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations%2C_1987.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_example_of_US_Federal_Environmental_Regulations,_1987.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Federal_Register" title="Federal Register">Federal Register</a> documents and literature related to US environmental regulations, including the <a href="/wiki/Resource_Conservation_and_Recovery_Act" title="Resource Conservation and Recovery Act">Resource Conservation and Recovery Act</a> (RCRA), 1987</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">Conservation movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_in_the_United_States" title="Conservation in the United States">Conservation in the United States</a></div>
<p>Environmental preservation in the United States and other parts of the world, including Australia, is viewed as the setting aside of natural resources to prevent damage caused by contact with humans or by certain human activities, such as logging, mining, hunting, and fishing, often to replace them with new human activities such as tourism and recreation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Regulations and laws may be enacted for the preservation of natural resources.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organisations_and_conferences">Organisations and conferences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Organisations and conferences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_environmental_organizations" title="List of environmental organizations">List of environmental organizations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty,_Madagascar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="367" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty%2C_Madagascar.jpg/220px-ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty%2C_Madagascar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty%2C_Madagascar.jpg/330px-ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty%2C_Madagascar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty%2C_Madagascar.jpg/440px-ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty%2C_Madagascar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ReefDoctor_work_station_in_Ifaty,_Madagascar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reef doctor work station in <a href="/wiki/Tsifota" title="Tsifota">Ifaty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a></div></div></div>
<p>Environmental organisations can be global, regional, national or local; they can be government-run or private (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGO" title="NGO">NGO</a>). Environmentalist activity exists in almost every country. Moreover, groups dedicated to community development and social justice also focus on environmental concerns.
</p><p>Some US environmental organisations, among them the <a href="/wiki/Natural_Resources_Defense_Council" title="Natural Resources Defense Council">Natural Resources Defense Council</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Defense_Fund" title="Environmental Defense Fund">Environmental Defense Fund</a>, specialise in bringing lawsuits (a tactic seen as particularly useful in that country). Other groups, such as the US-based <a href="/wiki/National_Wildlife_Federation" title="National Wildlife Federation">National Wildlife Federation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth_Day" title="Earth Day">Earth Day</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Cleanup_Day" title="National Cleanup Day">National Cleanup Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Nature_Conservancy" title="The Nature Conservancy">the Nature Conservancy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Wilderness_Society_(United_States)" title="The Wilderness Society (United States)">The Wilderness Society</a>, and global groups like the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friends_of_the_Earth" title="Friends of the Earth">Friends of the Earth</a>, disseminate information, participate in <a href="/wiki/Hearing_(law)" title="Hearing (law)">public hearings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobby</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demonstration_(people)" title="Demonstration (people)">stage demonstrations</a>, and may purchase land for <a href="/wiki/Nature_reserve" title="Nature reserve">preservation</a>. Statewide nonprofit organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/Wyoming_Outdoor_Council" title="Wyoming Outdoor Council">Wyoming Outdoor Council</a> often collaborate with these national organisations and employ similar strategies. Smaller groups, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wildlife_Conservation_International" title="Wildlife Conservation International">Wildlife Conservation International</a>, conduct research on <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a>. More radical organisations, such as <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth_First!" title="Earth First!">Earth First!</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Liberation_Front" title="Earth Liberation Front">Earth Liberation Front</a>, have more directly opposed actions they regard as environmentally harmful. While Greenpeace is devoted to nonviolent confrontation as a means of bearing witness to environmental wrongs and bringing issues into the public realm for debate, the underground <i>Earth Liberation Front</i> engages in the clandestine destruction of property, the release of caged or penned animals, and other criminal acts. Such tactics are regarded as unusual within the movement, however.
</p><p>On an international level, concern for the environment was the subject of a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_the_Human_Environment" title="United Nations Conference on the Human Environment">United Nations Conference on the Human Environment</a> in Stockholm in 1972, attended by 114 nations. Out of this meeting developed the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP) and the follow-up <a href="/wiki/Earth_Summit" title="Earth Summit">United Nations Conference on Environment and Development</a> in 1992. Other international organisations in support of environmental policies development include the <a href="/wiki/Commission_for_Environmental_Cooperation" title="Commission for Environmental Cooperation">Commission for Environmental Cooperation</a> (as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAFTA" title="NAFTA">NAFTA</a>), the <a href="/wiki/European_Environment_Agency" title="European Environment Agency">European Environment Agency</a> (EEA), and the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_protests">Environmental protests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Environmental protests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ClimateCampBAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ea/ClimateCampBAA.jpg/220px-ClimateCampBAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ea/ClimateCampBAA.jpg/330px-ClimateCampBAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/ea/ClimateCampBAA.jpg/440px-ClimateCampBAA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ClimateCampBAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Climate activists blockade <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Airports_Authority" title="British Airports Authority">British Airports Authority</a>'s headquarters for day of action.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3840" data-file-width="5760" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg/220px-March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg/330px-March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg/440px-March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:March_Against_Monsanto_Vancouver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/March_Against_Monsanto" title="March Against Monsanto">March Against Monsanto</a>", Vancouver, Canada, 25 May 2013</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmentalists">Environmentalists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Environmentalists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmentalist" title="Environmentalist">Environmentalist</a></div>
<p>Notable advocates for <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">environmental protection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> include:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r998391716" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 18em;">

<li><a href="/wiki/Lisa_P._Jackson" title="Lisa P. Jackson">Lisa P. Jackson</a> (chemical engineer and former administrator of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Naomi_Klein" title="Naomi Klein">Naomi Klein</a> (writer, activist)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Winona_LaDuke" title="Winona LaDuke">Winona LaDuke</a> (environmentalist)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aldo_Leopold" title="Aldo Leopold">Aldo Leopold</a> (ecologist)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/A._Carl_Leopold" title="A. Carl Leopold">A. Carl Leopold</a> (plant physiologist)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Lovelock" title="James Lovelock">James Lovelock</a> (scientist)</li>
