<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assassinations">Assassinations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Assassinations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GuyBradley1905.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1416" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/GuyBradley1905.jpg/220px-GuyBradley1905.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/GuyBradley1905.jpg/330px-GuyBradley1905.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/GuyBradley1905.jpg/440px-GuyBradley1905.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GuyBradley1905.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early American game warden <a href="/wiki/Guy_Bradley" title="Guy Bradley">Guy Bradley</a>, who was killed in 1905 while attempting to stop a bird poacher near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flamingo,_Florida" title="Flamingo, Florida">Flamingo, Florida</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_environmental_activists_assassinated" title="List of environmental activists assassinated">List of environmental activists assassinated</a></div>
<p>Every year, more than 100 environmental activists are murdered throughout the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-environmental_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-environmental-66">[66]</a></sup> Most recent deaths are in Brazil, where activists combat logging in the Amazon rainforest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>116 environmental activists were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assassinate" title="Assassinate">assassinated</a> in 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and 185 in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-environmental_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-environmental-66">[66]</a></sup> This represents more than two environmentalists assassinated every week in 2014 and three every week in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> More than 200 environmental activists were assassinated worldwide between 2016 and early 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> A 2020 incident saw several rangers murdered in the Congo Rainforest by poaching squads. Occurrences like this are relatively common, and account for a large number of deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_popular_culture" title="Climate change in popular culture">Climate change in popular culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism_in_music" title="Environmentalism in music">Environmentalism in music</a></div>
<ul><li>The U.S. Forest Service created <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smokey_the_Bear" title="Smokey the Bear">Smokey the Bear</a> in 1944; he appeared in countless posters, radio and television programs, movies, press releases, and other guises to warn about forest fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>The comic strip <i><a href="/wiki/Mark_Trail" title="Mark Trail">Mark Trail</a></i>, by environmentalist <a href="/wiki/Ed_Dodd" title="Ed Dodd">Ed Dodd</a>, began in 1946; it still appears weekly in 175 newspapers.</li>
<li>The children's animated show <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_Planet_and_the_Planeteers" title="Captain Planet and the Planeteers">Captain Planet and the Planeteers</a></i>, created by <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">Ted Turner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Pyle" title="Barbara Pyle">Barbara Pyle</a> in 1989 to inform children about environmental issues. The show aired for six seasons and 113 episodes, in 100 countries worldwide from 1990 to 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1974, <a href="/wiki/Spokane,_Washington" title="Spokane, Washington">Spokane</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_Washington" title="State of Washington">Washington</a>, became the smallest city ever to host a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World%27s_Fair" title="World's Fair">World's Fair</a>. From Saturday, 4 May, to Sunday, 3 November 1974, Spokane hosted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expo_74" title="Expo 74">Expo 74</a>, the first world's fair to focus on the environment. The theme of Expo 74 was "Celebrating Tomorrow's Fresh New Environment".</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/FernGully:_The_Last_Rainforest" title="FernGully: The Last Rainforest">FernGully: The Last Rainforest</a></i> is an animated motion picture released in 1992, which focuses exclusively on the environment. The movie is based on a book under the same title by Diana Young. In 1998, a sequel, <i><a href="/wiki/FernGully_2:_The_Magical_Rescue" title="FernGully 2: The Magical Rescue">FernGully 2: The Magical Rescue</a></i>, was introduced.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Miss_Earth" title="Miss Earth">Miss Earth</a> is one of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Four_international_beauty_pageants" title="Big Four international beauty pageants">Big Four international beauty pageants</a>. (The other three are <a href="/wiki/Miss_Universe" title="Miss Universe">Miss Universe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miss_International" title="Miss International">Miss International</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miss_World" title="Miss World">Miss World</a>.) Out of these four beauty pageants, Miss Earth is the only international beauty pageant that promotes "environmental awareness". The reigning titleholders dedicate their year to promote specific projects and often address issues concerning the environment and other global issues through school tours, tree planting activities, street campaigns, coastal clean ups, speaking engagements, shopping mall tours, media guesting, environmental fair, storytelling programs, eco-fashion shows, and other environmental activities. The Miss Earth winner is the spokesperson for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miss_Earth_Foundation" title="Miss Earth Foundation">Miss Earth Foundation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP) and other environmental organizations. The Miss Earth Foundation also works with the environmental departments and ministries of participating countries, various private sectors and corporations, as well as <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Foundation" title="World Wildlife Foundation">World Wildlife Foundation</a> (WWF).</li>
<li>Another area of environmentalism is to use art to raise awareness about misuse of the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> One example is <a href="/wiki/Trashion" title="Trashion">trashion</a>, using trash to create clothes, jewelry, and other objects for the home. <a href="/wiki/Marina_DeBris" title="Marina DeBris">Marina DeBris</a> is one trashion artist, who focuses on ocean and beach trash to design clothes and for fund raising, education.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_and_alternative_views">Criticism and alternative views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Environmentalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Criticism and alternative views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When environmentalism first became popular during the early 20th century, the focus was wilderness protection and wildlife preservation. These goals reflected the interests of the movement's initial, primarily white middle and upper class supporters, including through viewing preservation and protection via a lens that failed to appreciate the centuries-long work of indigenous communities who had lived without ushering in the types of environmental devastation these settler colonial "environmentalists" now sought to mitigate. The actions of many mainstream environmental organizations still reflect these early principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandler,_R._2007_pp._27-55_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandler,_R._2007_pp._27-55-78">[78]</a></sup> Numerous low-income minorities felt isolated or negatively impacted by the movement, exemplified by the Southwest Organizing Project's (SWOP) Letter to the Group of 10, a letter sent to major environmental organizations by several local environmental justice activists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The letter argued that the environmental movement was so concerned about cleaning up and preserving nature that it ignored the negative side-effects that doing so caused communities nearby, namely less job growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandler,_R._2007_pp._27-55_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sandler,_R._2007_pp._27-55-78">[78]</a></sup> In addition, the <a href="/wiki/NIMBY" title="NIMBY">NIMBY</a> movement has transferred <a href="/wiki/Locally_unwanted_land_use" title="Locally unwanted land use">locally unwanted land uses</a> (LULUs) from middle-class neighborhoods to poor communities with large minority populations. Therefore, vulnerable communities with fewer political opportunities are more often exposed to hazardous waste and toxins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIMBY_Victims_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIMBY_Victims-80">[80]</a></sup> This has resulted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PIBBY" title="PIBBY">PIBBY</a> principle, or at least the PIMBY (Place-in-minorities'-backyard), as supported by the United Church of Christ's study in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Civil_Rights_Learning_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Civil_Rights_Learning-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Environmental_elitism"></span>
As a result, some minorities have viewed the environmental movement as elitist. Environmental elitism manifested itself in three different forms:
</p>
<ol><li><i>Compositional</i> – Environmentalists are from the middle and upper class.</li>
<li><i>Ideological</i> – The reforms benefit the movement's supporters but impose costs on nonparticipants.</li>
<li><i>Impact</i> – The reforms have "regressive social impacts". They disproportionately benefit environmentalists and harm underrepresented populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Environmental_elitism_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Environmental_elitism-82">[82]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Many environmentalists believe that human interference with 'nature' should be restricted or minimised as a matter of urgency (for the sake of life, or the planet, or just for the benefit of the human species),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_skeptics" title="Environmental skeptics">environmental skeptics</a> and anti-environmentalists do not believe that there is such a need.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> One can also regard oneself as an environmentalist and believe that human 'interference' with 'nature' should be <i>increased</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Nevertheless, there is a risk that the shift from emotional environmentalism into the technical management of natural resources and hazards could decrease the touch of humans with nature, leading to less concern with environment preservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Increasingly, typical conservation rhetoric is being replaced with restoration approaches and larger landscape initiatives that seek to create more holistic impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2000s, American author and filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Michael_Crichton" title="Michael Crichton">Michael Crichton</a> criticized environmentalism as being religiously motivated rather than grounded in <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">empirical evidence</a>, arguing that <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> was a natural part of Earth's history and had been occurring long before humans dominated the planet. Also claiming to argue from his minor education in anthropology, he stated that religion was a part of human social make-up and that if it was suppressed, it would simply re-emerge in another form. With the decline of Christianity and Church attendance in the Western world, environmentalism has become more popular according to him, which he termed as "the religion of urban atheists".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Others seek a balance that involves both caring deeply for the environment while letting science guide human actions affecting it. Such an approach would avoid the emotionalism which, for example, anti-<a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">GMO</a> activism has been criticized for, and protect the integrity of science. Planting trees, for another example, can be emotionally satisfying but should also involve being conscious of scientific concerns such as the effect on <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycles</a> and the use of nonnative, potentially invasive species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>

Tucson. 246 pp.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Please supply an ISBN for this book.">ISBN missing</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adam_Tooze" title="Adam Tooze">Tooze, Adam</a>, "Democracy and Its Discontents", <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Review_of_Books" title="The New York Review of Books">The New York Review of Books</a></i>, vol. LXVI, no. 10 (6 June 2019), pp. 52–53, 56–57.  "Democracy has no clear answer for the mindless operation of <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucratic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technological power</a>.  We may indeed be witnessing its extension in the form of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a>.  Likewise, after decades of dire warning, the environmental problem remains fundamentally unaddressed.... Bureaucratic overreach and environmental catastrophe are precisely the kinds of slow-moving existential challenges that democracies deal with very badly.... Finally, there is the threat du jour:  <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporations</a> and the technologies they promote."  (pp. 56–57.)</li>
<li>Verweij, Marco; Thompson, Michael (eds), 2006, <i>Clumsy Solutions for a Complex World: Governance, Politics and Plural Perceptions</i>, Basingstoke: Palgrave Macmillan, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-230-00230-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-230-00230-2">978-0-230-00230-2</a></li>
<li>Vogel, David. <i>California Greenin': How the Golden State Became an Environmental Leader</i> (2018) 280 pp  <a class="external text" href="http://eh.net/?s=vogel+david" rel="nofollow">online review</a></li>
